    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8327371148892638364">In Darujhistan, the city of blue fire, it is said that love and death shall arrive dancing. It is summer and the heat is oppressive, but for the small round man in the faded red waistcoat, discomfiture is not just because of the sun. All is not well. Dire portents plague his nights and haunt the city streets like fiends of shadow. Assassins skulk in alleyways, but the quar</span>
  <span id="freeText8327371148892638364" style="display:none">In Darujhistan, the city of blue fire, it is said that love and death shall arrive dancing. It is summer and the heat is oppressive, but for the small round man in the faded red waistcoat, discomfiture is not just because of the sun. All is not well. Dire portents plague his nights and haunt the city streets like fiends of shadow. Assassins skulk in alleyways, but the quarry has turned and the hunters become the hunted. Hidden hands pluck the strings of tyranny like a fell chorus. While the bards sing their tragic tales, somewhere in the distance can be heard the baying of Hounds...And in the distant city of Black Coral, where rules Anomander Rake, Son of Darkness, ancient crimes awaken, intent on revenge. It seems Love and Death are indeed about to arrive...hand in hand, dancing. A thrilling, harrowing novel of war, intrigue and dark, uncontrollable magic, "Toll the Hounds" is the new chapter in Erikson's monumental series - epic fantasy at its most imaginative and storytelling at its most exciting.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8327371148892638364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/877232-should-i-skip-it-after-reading-multiple">Should I skip it??
After reading multiple relatively negative reviews for Toll the Hounds, I am a little reluctant. I am definitely a greater fan of the more plot-driven books than those that round out characters. Plus, it sounds like many reviewers think the last ~50 pages are what make the read worthwhile. 
So, what do you think of reading a synopsis +/– the last portion of the book?
Bad time-saving idea?</a>
</div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1830298704?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780765348852" href="/book/show/938534.Toll_the_Hounds">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1830298704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3324407191990554442"><b>Against all odds, <i>Toll the Hounds</i> blew my mind away and became one of my favorite installment within the series.</b><br/><br/><i>Toll the Hounds</i>, just like <i>House of Chains</i>, is one of the installments which I heard plenty of mixed things about; they’re there for valid reasons. However, unlike <i>House of Chains</i> which disappointed me a lot, I actually found <i>Toll the Hounds</i> mesmerizing, a treasure trove for philosophies, and also one of the most rewarding books in the series so far. The story of the novel focused on t</span>
  <span id="freeText3324407191990554442" style="display:none"><b>Against all odds, <i>Toll the Hounds</i> blew my mind away and became one of my favorite installment within the series.</b><br><br><i>Toll the Hounds</i>, just like <i>House of Chains</i>, is one of the installments which I heard plenty of mixed things about; they’re there for valid reasons. However, unlike <i>House of Chains</i> which disappointed me a lot, I actually found <i>Toll the Hounds</i> mesmerizing, a treasure trove for philosophies, and also one of the most rewarding books in the series so far. The story of the novel focused on the characters in Darujhistan and the Tiste Andii race. That’s right, we’re finally back in Darujhistan after seven books and we finally get to see the story focusing almost completely on the Tiste Andii.<br><br><b>Picture: A fanart of Anomander Rake by artsed-d8joaqa</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537455499i/26329534._SY540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I won’t lie, a lot of the warnings about the extremely slow pacing and the overload of philosophical content—as amazing as they were—towards this entry were there for good reasons. It took a LONG time to get to the rewarding convergence and believe me, this isn’t by all mean a flawless book; a lot of the philosophical content didn’t add any value to the book other than being there for social commentary. Like the majority of the fans said, the barrage of philosophies was so extreme that it almost damaged the entirety of the book. Every character has become a philosopher here; a single question asked will be answered after four or five paragraphs of self-contemplation. But gods below, the last 25% of the book was totally worth the struggle. Plus, I’m confident that this is a book that will be much better upon a reread due to the touch of omniscient narrative that Erikson added for premonition and foreshadowing value.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“People don’t change to suit their god; they change their god to suit them.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>The last quarter alone won’t be a sufficient reason to warrant my high rating and love for this book. Let’s start with expectation. This is probably the first time I’m thanking all the warning provided by the fans of the series; without spoilers too! If I haven’t been warned about what kind of pacing to expect here, I most likely would suffer through this read a LOT more. This raised the question of whether the book was actually good or not that it required a warning to be enjoyed but I won’t get into that now. Let’s just say that because of the word of mouth I received regarding this book, I ended up reading all the slow and philosophical contents as a lesson in writings and a dissection of philosophies instead. This made my reading experience much less boring than it was supposed to be.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“Survivors do not mourn together. They each mourn alone, even when in the same place. Grief is the most solitary of all feelings. Grief isolates, and every ritual, every gesture, every embrace, is a hopeless effort to break through that isolation.<br>None of it works. The forms crumble and dissolve.<br>To face death is to stand alone.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>Talking about studying Erikson’s prose and storytelling structure within this book, I really need to applaud how well-crafted some of the chapters were. As I mentioned before, Erikson added an omniscient narrative to this book, I DISLIKED omniscient narrative, it very rarely worked for me. That being said, the new storytelling direction allowed Erikson’s prose to have freedom in switching POV from a bird's eye view to the standard close third person view. At the same time, Erikson was capable of telling an undiminished epic fantasy quality while effectively portraying what the characters felt with minimum words. In my opinion, one of the most brilliant writing skill in this book was the way Erikson starts and ends his chapter. For example, he starts one chapter with this sentence:<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“The soul knows no greater anguish than to take a breath that begins with love and ends with grief.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>And then he proceeds to tell an entire chapter revolving around that passage and finally ending it with the same passage added with discussions and philosophies that made the chapter more meaningful. This situation happened several times and they were astonishingly brilliant.<br><br>Other than readers’ warning, the other crucial aspect on why I found the extremely slow pacing tolerable was due to the familiarity of settings and characters. This doesn’t mean that there weren’t any new characters to find here, Erikson just won’t simplify things for readers. The main difference between this book and the previous installment was that I found the new characters in this novel to be so much better to follow than the one in <i>Reaper’s Gale</i>. There weren’t any ridiculously dumb and pointless story arc just like Redmask or the Shake (forgot his/her name already due to boredom) storyline. In fact, Harllo was one of the few characters Erikson wrote that made me truly empathize with. For god sake, Harllo was practically Fitz plunged into the Malazan world, enough said.<br><br>Throughout the majority of the book—give or take 75%—the plot looked like it’s going nowhere and I seriously wondered “what the hell is Erikson trying to do here?”, “what even is the true main plot of the novel?” and “how will he be able to conclude this one satisfyingly!?” and Beru fends, he did. He absolutely nailed the last 25% with massive impact force; making the last 25% of the book the best explosive closing in the series so far; even better than the climax sequences in <i>Memories of Ice</i>. It was astonishing and ingenious the way Erikson unraveled everything he has prepared into the last 25% of the story. I wish I could give you a spoiler-free explanation on how masterful it was but I can’t. I’m honestly speechless and I’m writing this with the convergence playing like a movie in my head. It was truly a cinematic experience of grief, darkness, light, vengeance, ash, destruction, humor, and of course, one of the greatest imagery that epic fantasy has to offer; you must experience this for yourself.<br><br><b>Picture: One of the interior artworks in the Subterranean edition by Marc Simonetti</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537455499i/26329535._SY540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I’m at the homestretch of the series now and I don’t really have anything else to say about this tome without going into repetition or spoiler territory. <i>Toll the Hounds</i> may have a lot of flaws in pacing and overload of philosophical contents—I love philosophies but anything that’s too much is never good—but overall, it was still expertly crafted. Only two more books to go now. The beginning of the end has been sparked and my journey within this gigantic series is very close to the end. Allow me to close this review with a quote that will definitely stick with me for a long time.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“There is no struggle too vast, no odds too overwhelming, for even should we fail - should we fall - we will know that we have lived.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>Or in other words of my other absolute favorite series: <b>
  <i>“Journey before destination.”</i>
</b><br><br><b>You can order the book <u><a href="http://www.bookdepository.com/book/9780553824469/?a_aid=petrikleo" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE!</a></u></b><br><br><i>You can find <b><u><a href="http://novelnotions.net/2018/09/20/toll-the-hounds/" rel="nofollow noopener">this</a></u></b> and the rest of my reviews at <b><u><a href="http://novelnotions.net" rel="nofollow noopener">Novel Notions</a></u></b></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="3324407191990554442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1204744997" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1204744997" />
    <a title="TS Chan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30279227-ts-chan"><img alt="TS Chan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543800303p2/30279227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1204744997?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TS Chan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TS Chan" href="/user/show/30279227-ts-chan">TS Chan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781409083313" href="/book/show/13449914-toll-the-hounds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30279227-ts-chan?shelf=malazan">malazan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30279227-ts-chan?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30279227-ts-chan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30279227-ts-chan?shelf=dr-reading-challenge">dr-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1204744997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14979455716307719126"><b>In a series replete with epic endings, <i>Toll the Hounds</i> offered, to date, the most epic one of all. It is with much joy that upon rereading I could upgrade this book as one of my favourites.</b> <br/><br/>Before I start my review proper, I’d like to point out that reading <i>Return of the Crimson Guards</i> from the <i>Malazan Empire</i> series prior to Toll the Hounds is relevant to the timeline, both from an event and character standpoint. <br/><br/>The plot in <i>Toll the Hounds</i> seemingly digressed from the central arc of the Cripple</span>
  <span id="freeText14979455716307719126" style="display:none"><b>In a series replete with epic endings, <i>Toll the Hounds</i> offered, to date, the most epic one of all. It is with much joy that upon rereading I could upgrade this book as one of my favourites.</b> <br><br>Before I start my review proper, I’d like to point out that reading <i>Return of the Crimson Guards</i> from the <i>Malazan Empire</i> series prior to Toll the Hounds is relevant to the timeline, both from an event and character standpoint. <br><br>The plot in <i>Toll the Hounds</i> seemingly digressed from the central arc of the Crippled God and was no less captivating because of it. While the past few books gave readers an in-depth view of the Tiste Edur of Shadow, this one brought focus to the Tiste Andii of Darkness, of whom we were only given small morsels of information so far. A mysterious race, given to excessive introspection and deep melancholy, the Andii is led by an utterly enigmatic and supremely great leader, the Son of Darkness. There is still so much fascinating history to be discovered about the Tiste Andii that Erikson’s current series, <i>The Kharkanas Trilogy</i>, is being written to address this. <br><br>Anomander Rake, Lord of the Tiste Andii, Son of Darkness, is in my view, one of the greatest fantasy characters that I’ve ever encountered. Even more amazingly, is how Erikson elevated him to such lofty ideals without ever having his own point-of-view, as if the very enigma from doing so made him ever more compelling and charismatic. What we did get were the viewpoints of other characters, be it his people or other races or even gods and ascendants, and their highest regards for him.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506052429i/23983253._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>
  <b>
    <blockquote>The chaos in you, yes, a fire on the promontory, a beacon piercing the profound entropy we saw all around us. And yet, so few of you proved worthy of our allegiance. So few, Lord, and fewer with each generation, until now here you stand, virtually alone. <br><br>The one who was worth it. The only one.</blockquote>
  </b>
</i><br>There is much skill in creating beautiful yet subtle symmetry in storytelling. A skill that Erikson has in spades as evident from his narrative. <i>Toll the Hounds</i> can be called as the penultimate volume in <i>Malazan Book of the Fallen</i> as the next two books, <i>Dust of Dreams</i> and <i>The Crippled God</i>, form a two-part finale. This vast, sweeping and intricate tale begun in Darujhistan in <i>Gardens of the Moon</i> and had now come full circle with a convergence of mind-boggling proportions. The word 'epic' will not even begin to describe the confluence of almost all the major, or biggest, or most badass characters, descending upon the unwitting citizens of the City of Blue Fire. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506087615i/23987686._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>I cannot sufficiently praise the brilliant writing commanded by Erikson to tell the tale of this fateful city, in the form of the most engaging and unique voice of that little round man in a red waistcoat, Kruppe. The storytelling literally serenades the reader with a combination of poetic, elegant, graceful and, at times, alliterative turn of phrases. <br><br><i>
  <b>
    <blockquote>Arrivals. Glory and portent, delightful reunion and terrible imminence, winged this and winged that and escapes and releases and pending clashes and nefarious demands for recompense over a single mouthful of spat wine, such a night! Such a night! <br>----------------------------------<br>And this was a night like any other, a skein of expectations and anticipations, revelations and perturbations. Look around. Look around! On all sides, day and night, light and dark! Every step taken with the firm resolve to believe in the solid ground awaiting it. Every step, one after another, again and again, and no perilous ledge yawns ahead, oh no. Step and step, now, step and step. </blockquote>
  </b>
</i><br>The ratings for <i>Toll the Hounds</i> were probably among the most divergent of this series, as the pace was very slow for a significant part of the book with a greater level of dense philosophical commentary and seemingly overindulgent introspection. In fact, my initial read suffered because of this aspect. However, being in the right state of mind as I tackled my reread and having gained a newfound appreciation for deep ruminations, I experienced a significant improvement in my level of enjoyment while reading this tome. From themes of mortality and morality; servitude, sacrifice and salvation; redemption and restitution – it was so pervasive and pondering in the narrative that I do acknowledge that pacing suffers but I was so thoroughly immersed in it this time that it no longer was an issue. <br> <br>While the climaxes of the preceding books tend to commence around the last ten percent or with two main chapters remaining, we are treated to a doubly extended climactic ending in this volume. This was due to the presence of more subplots and arcs in the narrative; all masterfully intertwined towards the aforementioned convergence that was most poignantly expressed in the voice of Kruppe. And the ending set the stage for the two-part finale to come.<br><br><i>Malazan Book of the Fallen</i> is so named for its heart-wrenching and tragic tales. These span from the small and personal ones to the immense and epic. Was all this grief and suffering for nought? For in the words of one of the greatest of all, <br><br><i>
  <b>
    <blockquote>'There is no struggle too vast, no odds too overwhelming, for even should we fail - should we fall - we will know that we have lived.'</blockquote>
  </b>
</i><br><br><strong>You can purchase the book from <u><a href="https://www.bookdepository.com/Toll-Hounds-Steven-Erikson/9780553824469/?a_aid=petrikleo" rel="nofollow noopener">Book Depository (Free Shipping)</a></u> | <u><a href="https://bookshop.org/books/toll-the-hounds/9780765348852" rel="nofollow noopener">Bookshop.Org</a></u></strong><br><br><i>You can find <a href="https://wp.me/pa0WBY-1Mb" rel="nofollow noopener">this</a> and my other reviews at <a href="http://novelnotions.net/category/book-reviews/tss-reviews/" rel="nofollow noopener">Novel Notions.</a></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="14979455716307719126" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2080032688" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2080032688" />
    <a title="Jody " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41767362-jody"><img alt="Jody " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558120407p2/41767362.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2080032688?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jody " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jody " href="/user/show/41767362-jody">Jody </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780765348852" href="/book/show/938534.Toll_the_Hounds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41767362-jody?shelf=malazan">malazan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41767362-jody?shelf=grim-dark">grim-dark</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2080032688" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11495187308793043583">It has been over a week since I finished <b>
  <i>Toll the Hounds</i>
</b>, so I have had plenty of time to think about how I wanted to review this. Personally, I thought this was the weakest book in the series. Now, before you go all berserk on me I have my reasons, and let me emphasis that while I say it is the weakest book in the series it is also a great book by any other standards. But putting it up against all the other Malazan books it just fell a little short for me. Let me explain!<br/><br/>There were a lot of</span>
  <span id="freeText11495187308793043583" style="display:none">It has been over a week since I finished <b>
  <i>Toll the Hounds</i>
</b>, so I have had plenty of time to think about how I wanted to review this. Personally, I thought this was the weakest book in the series. Now, before you go all berserk on me I have my reasons, and let me emphasis that while I say it is the weakest book in the series it is also a great book by any other standards. But putting it up against all the other Malazan books it just fell a little short for me. Let me explain!<br /><br />There were a lot of sections in the book were not much happened. With books this size I understand there isn’t going to be action from beginning to end, and I am used to the (non-combatant interludes) in these books, but it just got to me in this one. I actually felt a lack of interest at certain times, and I believe this contributed to me taking so long to finish it. I just had no desire to pick it up as much as I did the other books. Maybe it’s just Malazan overload from the past few months, but I don’t think so. <br /><br />Also, I had a hard time figuring out exactly what was going on in some areas of the story. Erikson has a way of befuddling the mind sometimes, and I found that happening a lot to me in this book. I think back to some scenes and still have no clue what he was trying to achieve. Now, knowing how Erikson writes, this may all come to pass with the next two books and I will probably be sticking my foot in my mouth by the end of the series, but until then….<br /> <br />OK! Now that I have gotten my complaints out of the way let us move on to the positive side of the review and other thoughts. <br /><br />I was so glad to be back in the city of Darujhistan and with characters that got me to fall in love with this series way back in GotM. There is Murillio, Anomander Rake, and of course the linguistic genius who is always a step ahead of everyone else, Kruppe.<br /> <br /><b>
  <i>‘Whatever happened to common decency? To decent commonry? What’s decent is rarely common – that is true enough, while the obverse is perverse in all prickly irony, would you not agree? Kruppe would, being such an agreeable sort.’</i>
</b><br /><br />While there were other characters I would like to mention, I can’t due to spoilers for those of you who haven’t made it this far yet. <br /><br />Erikson has a way of tying in events or little phrases that relate back to previous books that I really enjoy. A joke between characters, a dark omen that finally came to pass, or some reference back to an event that you just hope to catch. This makes these books enjoyable to read, but can also seem impossible to notice with the amount of information in each book. Here is one I happened to stumble upon. See if you can see the relation back to GotM.<br /> <br /><b>
  <i>‘Love found and love lost. A certain desperation that still spins like a coin – which way will it fall? Question as yet unanswered, a future as yet undecided.’</i>
</b><br /><br />As with all Malazan books you’re going to have your share of heartache, and a great convergence at least once in every book. These two things are sometimes intertwined and other times not. While I enjoyed the convergence in this one it was definitely overshadowed by the heartache I felt. It is not uncommon to have these emotions battle with each other in this series, but in this book it was no contest.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>‘The soul knows no great anguish than to take a breath that begins with love and ends with grief.’</i>
</b><br /><br />I know I said earlier this is the weakest book in the series, and I will stand by that. There are always going to be weaker books in any series. Having said that, I did enjoy this way more than it frustrated me. I was definitely caught off guard by the problems I had with it, and I think that is why I feel the need to explain my complaints as much as I have. Toll the Hounds is a vital addition to this series, and one I am sure I will look back on as an intricate piece of this vast Malazan puzzle when I am done. Sadly, it did not make my 5 star cut, but of course I encourage all of you to read it and decide for yourselves.<br /><br />4 stars ****</span>
  <a data-text-id="11495187308793043583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2309354266" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2309354266" />
    <a title="Deborah Obida" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54790180-deborah-obida"><img alt="Deborah Obida" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604679377p2/54790180.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2309354266?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Obida" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Obida" href="/user/show/54790180-deborah-obida">Deborah Obida</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553813197" href="/book/show/3952378-toll-the-hounds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54790180-deborah-obida?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54790180-deborah-obida?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2309354266" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6651370321705594406"><b>
  <i>"There is no struggle too vast, no odds too overwhelming,<br/> for even should we fail – should we fall<br/> – we will know that we have lived."<br/>Anomander Rake<br/> Son of Darkness</i>
</b><br/><br/>I have just one problem with this book, and that is its slowness, this book is so damn slow, this book could have been better if it was 500 pages shorter, I will read and read yet nothing is happening, it made the reading experience not as fun as the previous ones, I have to admit I was bored at some point, which is the first time</span>
  <span id="freeText6651370321705594406" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>"There is no struggle too vast, no odds too overwhelming,<br /> for even should we fail – should we fall<br /> – we will know that we have lived."<br />Anomander Rake<br /> Son of Darkness</i>
</b><br /><br />I have just one problem with this book, and that is its slowness, this book is so damn slow, this book could have been better if it was 500 pages shorter, I will read and read yet nothing is happening, it made the reading experience not as fun as the previous ones, I have to admit I was bored at some point, which is the first time since I started this series.<br />The unnecessary descriptions of morning and evenings got tiring after the first few times, I started skimming the rest, don’t even get me started on the weather. It would have been better if he had adopted the writing style of GoTM or MT, that a part is dedicated to one location, then they all meet at the final part.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Speak truth, grow still, until the water is<br /> clear between us.<br />Meditations of the Tiste Andii</i>
</b><br /><br />Moving on to things I love in this book. One we are back to Darujhistan, we’ve heard almost nothing from this city since GoTM, the first book, another location is Black Coral which is now ruled by the Anomander, so the Andiis live there with some humans. We also have few other locations and some new characters which would be discussed in the character section.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Survivors do not mourn together. They each mourn alone, even when in the same place. Grief is the most solitary of all feelings. Grief isolates, and every ritual, every gesture, every embrace, is a hopeless effort to break through that isolation.</i>
</b><br /><br /><u>Characters</u><br /><i>If you are spoiler sensitive or haven’t read the first 7 books, I suggest you jump this section, cause even though I won’t mention spoilers I would mention character names which would tell you who made it this far in the series.</i><br /><br /><b>Darujhistan</b><br />Here we have Kruppe,Murillio,Coll, Rallick Nom and the rest of the usuals of Pheonix inn. Cutter and Torvald Nom are back to the city,with Scillara, Barathol and Chaur. Stonny now resides there and the remaining retired Bridgeburners that own K’rul’s bar and finally Baruk and Lady Envy.<br /><br />Nothing much happened with those at Phoenix inn, just the usual, the rest is spoilers, as usual politics and fight for council seat is as hot as ever, an asshole named Gorlas Vidikas really caused trouble for my loved characters.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Gorlas killed Murillio in a duel but Cutter killed him, Cutter is so badass now but still love and misses Apsalar, she sent him a message and told him to come met her <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Cutter is as lost as ever, also very pessimistic, Barathol is trying to start a new life same as Scillara. The bridgeburners have some assassin issues, those scenes were one if my favourite in the whole book. Stonny part wasn’t that fun, she was more bitchy than before.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Sacrifice must be weighed by the pain of what is surrendered, and this alone was the true measure of a virtue's worth.</i>
</b><br /><br /><u>Black Coral</u><br />To say the Tiste Andiis are hopeless is an understatement, Mother Dark’s rejection made them all turn to my beloved Anomander for everything, the weight on his shoulders, no wonder he is the way he is. So glad he has people like Spinnock Durav an Andii who would do anything for him, even die if Anomander asks *tears* and Endest Silann, just get ready to have watery eyes or tear up anytime you read his part, its just so sad. The ending part for these three is just so...., I just can’t find an appropriate word to describe it. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Anomander sacrificed himself and died to give his people hope, since he is the gate to Kurald Galain, he merged his soul with that of the gate in Dragnipur and made Black Coral a gate to their realm *wipes tears* Spinnock distracted Karllor from getting there to steal the sword and almost died, Endest Silann also helped stopped a dying god from taking over the Andiiis who wanted to pry on their vulnerability when Anomander was doing this. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /> <br />People now worship Itkovian and calls him the redeemer, as a new god things are not so easy, some bastards try to pry on that, the rest is spoilers.<br /><br /><u>Others</u><br />My beloved Nimander and co, Skintick, Destra, Nenanda, Aranatha and Kedaviss, how I love this lot. Nimander is so humble and has a low self esteem, I don’t blame him, life hasn’t exactly being kind to him, but despite this he is noble and is willing to die for his love ones, even strangers if need be, no wonder they follow and love him.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>'"Emotions are the spawn of true motivations, whether those motivations be conscious or otherwise",' said Nimander.</i>
</b><br /><br />Karsa, Samar and Traveller made this book more fun, the dialogue between Karsa and Samar is one of one favourites in the book, and guess what they are also heading to Darujhistan.<br /><br />Gruntle, Mappo and the Trygalle trade guild were in this but their parts was the slowest and boring, if not for the dialogue between the Bole brothers I might have skimmed these parts.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>'It's against the law to look in someone else's bag.'<br />'Swamp law. Does this look like a swamp?'<br />'That doesn't matter. You broke the law. Anyway, what you found was my spare moccasin.'<br />'Your one spare moccasin?'<br />'That's right.'<br />'Then why was it full of my love notes?'<br />'What love notes?'<br />'The ones me and her been writing back and forth. The ones I hid in my moccasin. Those ones, Jula.' <br />'What's obvious now is just how many times you been breaking the law. Because you been hiding your love notes – which you write to yourself and nobody else – you been hiding them in my spare moccasin!'<br />'Not that you'd ever look.'<br />'But I might, if I knew about it.'<br />'You didn't though, did you? Besides, you don't have a spare moccasin, because I stole it.'<br />'And that's why I stole it back!'<br />'You can't steal back what you didn't know was stolen in the first place. That's just stealing. And stealing's against the law.'<br />'Swamp law.'</i>
</b><br /><br />Harllo,<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Harllo is the son of Stonny, she got pregnant after that rape in Capustan, she named him after Harllo her friend <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>Bainisk and Bellam Nom are new characters that have managed to crip into my heart and top my favourites. <br /><br /><u>Plot</u><br />There is a prophecy about a convergence in Darujhistan that would involve Anomander, Lady Envy and Spite her sister, The hounds of Shadows and Light, Karsa, Samar, Traveller, Iskaral Pust and many others.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13821210487564464583"><b>Epilogue:</b> There is no bell that could toll for me now.*<br/><br/><b>Chapter 24:</b> She was there!   <span id="freeText13821210487564464583" style="display:none"><b>Epilogue:</b> There is no bell that could toll for me now.*<br /><br /><b>Chapter 24:</b> She was there! All the time! <br /><br /><b>Chapter 23:</b> Rake, dear me Rake…<br /><br /><b>Chapter 22:</b> What an army, what an <i>epic</i> army: Bridgeburners on the tip, Dujek on the left and Wickans on the right flank then Brukhalian’s Grey Swords. Next time I am moving countries, I am contracting Trygalle Trade Guild. Anybody’s got their contact details?<br /><br /><b>Chapter 2:</b> As your resident Tiste Andii in disguise, I think I could move to Black Coral any <s>day</s>night. (Ps remember, sun kills, think of the Sahara desert!)<br /><br /><b>Chapter 1:</b> Attention for POV change. I swear there are more POVs in this one chapter than in some whole fantasy series.<br /><br /><b>Prologue:</b> Oh no... only three books left...<br /><br /><b>* I am not going to even pretend that I could in any way give a pale shadow of justice to this book or indeed a whole series in a proper review.</b> There are many better than me, here on Goodreads (and many of those I'm lucky to have among my friends), who managed to grasp and convey the sheer brilliance of what awaits those who open the Malazan Book of the Fallen. <b>All I can give you is a chapter breakdown of my own ponderings for whom the bell tolls.</b><br /><br />The Malazan Book of the Fallen:<br /><br />1. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1577764482" rel="nofollow noopener">Gardens of the Moon</a> ★★★★★<br />2. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2277757848" rel="nofollow noopener">Deadhouse Gates</a> ★★★★★<br />3. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2379526635" rel="nofollow noopener">Memories of Ice</a> ★★★★★<br />4. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2437981495" rel="nofollow noopener">House of Chains</a> ★★★★★<br />5. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2516054197" rel="nofollow noopener">Midnight Tides</a> ★★★★★<br />6. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2596107471" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bonehunters</a> ★★★★★<br />7. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2652025092" rel="nofollow noopener">Reaper's Gale</a> ★★★★☆<br />9. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2905503793?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Dust of Dreams</a> ★★★☆☆ (and the third star is a testament to my generosity)<br />10. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2972632540" rel="nofollow noopener">The Crippled God</a> ★★☆☆☆</span>

<div id="review_2204988772" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2204988772" />
    <a title="Gavin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8192509-gavin"><img alt="Gavin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338207884p2/8192509.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2204988772?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gavin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gavin" href="/user/show/8192509-gavin">Gavin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8192509-gavin?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2204988772" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer920691274750294512">This was another good instalment of Steven Erikson's fantastic Malazan series. It was a step up in quality from what we got in Reaper's Gale. Mostly because I think the characters and balance of the story were better in Toll the Hounds! <br/><br/>As always I enjoyed Erikson's weird but engaging writing style and enjoyed his observations and scathing social commentary. <br/><br/>The setting of this instalment was a big plus for me as we returned to the city of Darujistan. It ranks up there with Letheras as my favour</span>
  <span id="freeText920691274750294512" style="display:none">This was another good instalment of Steven Erikson's fantastic Malazan series. It was a step up in quality from what we got in Reaper's Gale. Mostly because I think the characters and balance of the story were better in Toll the Hounds! <br /><br />As always I enjoyed Erikson's weird but engaging writing style and enjoyed his observations and scathing social commentary. <br /><br />The setting of this instalment was a big plus for me as we returned to the city of Darujistan. It ranks up there with Letheras as my favourite Malazan setting and has the added bonus of having a ton of great characters living in the city. The pick of the bunch being the always entertaining Kruppe, who managed to find himself sharing narration duties with Erikson for some of this story! <br /><br />Others big hitters who popped up in this story were Rake, Karsa, Traveller, Kallor, Envy, Spite, and the always fun duo of Shadowthrone and Cotillion. Not that the mortals were lacking as we got the return of old favourites like Crokus, Kruppe, Rallick Nom, Picker, Blend, and even Iskeral Pust! Plus we got to meet a bunch of fun new characters as well. The pick of the new guys being Harllo and Endest. <br /><br />I was not a giant fan of the super bleak tragic tone that ran through the story but Erikson just about managed to offset this with offering a few moments of triumph to go with the tragedy and by countering the tragedy with a story arc focusing on redemption. The tone was also lightened a bit with a some humor! The balance worked OK for me. Though I must admit I'm surprised a few of the characters never off'ed themselves before the big Convergence finally hit given how depressed and suicidal they were! <br /><br />After a near year long break from the Malazan series I found the start of this one a little hard to get into in the early stages. Erikson has a unique and weird writing style and it took me a little bit of time to acclimatize myself with it and get sucked back into the story. It likely did not help that I had to try and remember who some of the lesser characters were again. Luckily by the 20% to 25% mark I was back into the flow of all things Malazan! <br /><br />If I have to moan about something it is the fact that a few of the BIG deaths lacked the same level of emotional impact as a few of the very minor ones. That was a little disappointing. <br /><br />Darujistan, the Tiste Andii, Dragnipur, and that one big epic surprise death were the strength of this instalment. <br /><br />Rating: 4.5 stars. I always feel a little harsh not giving some of these Malazan stories a full 5 star rating! I think that is mostly because I tend to judge them against the best books in the series like Memories of Ice and Midnight Tides. So this is a 4.5 Malazan rating which equates to a solid 5 star rating in regular terms:)<br /><br />Audio Note: Michael Page has done a decent job with this series since taking over from the equally good Ralph Lister. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="920691274750294512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2301384163" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2301384163" />
    <a title="Stefan Bach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24053713-stefan-bach"><img alt="Stefan Bach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603215087p2/24053713.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2301384163?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stefan Bach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stefan Bach" href="/user/show/24053713-stefan-bach">Stefan Bach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781409083313" href="/book/show/13449914-toll-the-hounds">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2301384163" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11139149735390759944"><i>
  <b>“History meant nothing, because the only continuity was human stupidity.”</b>
</i><br/><br/>Where to even begin? <br/>From that magnificent ending? No, who starts with an ending?<br/>But in many ways, <i>
  <b>Toll the Hounds,</b>
</i> eight book in <i>
  <b>Malazan Book of the Fallen</b>
</i> series is the beginning of an end. Interlude into a final book <i>
  <b>Dust of Dreams/Crippled God;</b>
</i> book by which end all pieces are set and ready for a conclusion. <br/><br/>Let's start with the necessary - but I promise very brief - what, where and who.<br/><br/>We're back in <i>
  </i></span>
  <span id="freeText11139149735390759944" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>“History meant nothing, because the only continuity was human stupidity.”</b>
</i><br><br>Where to even begin? <br>From that magnificent ending? No, who starts with an ending?<br>But in many ways, <i>
  <b>Toll the Hounds,</b>
</i> eight book in <i>
  <b>Malazan Book of the Fallen</b>
</i> series is the beginning of an end. Interlude into a final book <i>
  <b>Dust of Dreams/Crippled God;</b>
</i> book by which end all pieces are set and ready for a conclusion. <br><br>Let's start with the necessary - but I promise very brief - what, where and who.<br><br>We're back in <i>
  <b>Darujhistan,</b>
</i> place where inevitable convergence is approaching. Convergence of characters, of destinies, past and future, old and new, friends and enemies, lovers, ascendants, gods, mules - all coming to a single crossroad.<br><br>And that's not the only convergence in this book. Deep within the sword, <i>
  <b>Chaos</b>
</i> and <i>
  <b>Dark</b>
</i> are about to battle each other to extinction, possibly ending the world and all realms in it.<br>The only force that is willing to oppose this imminent end is that which is bound and shackled to a wooden wagon, forces gathered through millennia, consisting of mutual dreaded enemies.<br><br><i>
  <b>
    <u>Characters.</u>
  </b>
</i><br><br>Reading this book reaffirmed my opinion that all-powerful characters, such as <i>
  <b>Anomander Rake,</b>
</i> hurts the overall quality of the story. <br>Yes, you have read those lines properly. Bear with me.<br><br>It's not that I hate having these types of characters as much is failing to see what other readers found so compelling in him and characters alike. <br>I mean, I get it, he is all-powerful, veers into a dragon, have a badass sword, he is also profoundly mysterious and unfathomably tragic. <br>And most of the time, that’s more than enough, because this is fantasy and something like this is presumable, right?<br><br>But, at some point, this vast power, this larger than life part in the story, becomes a burden to a character. Because whenever he comes into a spotlight, readers expect <i>"oooohh's"</i> and <i>"wwoooow's"</i> from him. They expect to be awestruck. <br>And that ultimately puts limitation on author himself, because there are expectations to be met and when author realizes he can't meet those expectations, like Erikson realized here*, they have none other choice than to put these type of characters on a shelf. To wait their opportunity to shine. <br><br><i>*(Because would you believe it that Steven never intended for Anomander to have a big part in this series? Actually, Erikson himself was quite confused that Anomandaris was received so well by readers because, like myself, he wasn’t infatuated by him at all. Yeah, shocking, I know. <br>But of course, because he writes for us, he gave us what we want. Well, what most of us want, in any case.)</i><br><br>And if author doesn't have other compelling stories – or if he simply relies too much on a story of this magnificent character – we are bound to read endlessly: <i>"No, it's not the time; no it's not the time; and no, it's still not the time."</i> throughout much of the 1200 pages of a book. Like in this one here.<br><br>Honestly, at some point I heard <i>
  <b>Jaffar</b>
</i> saying: <i>“Patience, <b>Iago,</b> patience.”</i><br><br>If other characters have to wait this one character; if other stories have to wait this one story to progress further – than that, in my opinion, hurts the quality of the story.<br>And this is exactly what happened here. Other stories suffered because of it; stories such as <i>
  <b>Cutter/Crocus</b>
</i> and <i>
  <b>Chalice D’Arle</b>
</i> which we could completely cut off, and except 80 pages of the book, we wouldn’t lose much at all. <br>Add on that <i>
  <b>Rallick Nom</b>
</i> and his cousin <i>
  <b>Torvald Nom.</b>
</i> Add even <i>
  <b>Envy</b>
</i> and <i>
  <b>Spite.</b>
</i> I would even go so far to cut off <i>
  <b>Mapo</b>
</i> and <i>
  <b>Gruntle’s</b>
</i> story. <br>All these stories, which served as an underwhelming supplement while we’re waiting – well, we don’t know what exactly is that we’re waiting – all we know is that: <i>“It’s almost the time.”,</i> I would completely cut off, saving approximately 400 pages, without losing any thread whatsoever.<br><br><i>
  <b>“Perspective, you see? The world changes according to where you stand.”</b>
</i><br><br>Which leads us to last 150/200 pages, which were breathless. Something we’re quite used to seeing in Steven’s books and something that doesn’t surprise us anymore. <br>Now obviously I can’t talk in details about it, but I’ll mention this: the main reason for me why last 200 pages kept me on the edge of my seat is mainly because of the prequels – story and character I’m following from them, all throughout this series, and seeing revelations and seemingly conclusion to that character’s arc. In all of my previous reads I wasn’t as invested as in this one, with all the prequels, as well as side-novels of this world, backing me up.<br>Just because of it, again, I’ll recommend chronological reading order for anyone that has nerves, time and overall desire to do so.<br><br><i>
  <b>“To face death is to stand alone.”</b>
</i><br><i>– Preparation for character’s death. –</i><br><br>I love the fact that Steven was considerate enough to groom us and prepare for what is to come; for this emotional barrage and sad departure from characters that we love.<br>In the story we have a character who dies. With Steven Erikson that’s not even a spoiler, it’s something that we expect in each book, at every corner to jump right at us.<br>But the way he had done it, through seemingly insignificant character and within a minor story in the book, is a masterful preparation for his readers for what is to come by the end.<br><br><i>
  <b>"Sadness belonged. As rightful as joy, love, grief and fear. All conditions of being.<br>Too often people mistook the sadness in others for self-pity, and in so doing revealed their own hardness of spirit, and more than a little malice."</b>
</i><br><br>Focusing so much and in so many details on this minor story and this insignificant character, and to let us grieve alongside these character’s friends, feeling their pain, paying respect to their fallen comrade, going through all these stages, denial, anger, grief and finally acceptance, actually helped in accepting what is inevitably to come, on our feet, with grace.<br>Brilliance is in small details.<br><br><i>
  <b>
    <u>Symbolism and allegory.</u>
  </b>
</i><br><br><i>
  <b>"People don’t change to suit their god; they change their god to suit them."</b>
</i><br><br>What do you do when god refuses to acknowledge you?<br>Would you transform your faith in desperate need for answers? Would you pick an easier way to find whatever meaning you can? <br>What then hides behind your prayer? When the purpose of the prayer becomes seeking of hope or a blessing, or the sense that something greater than yourself might acknowledge your helplessness – all of which in order to serve your wants and needs – does a prayer becomes a meaning of reciprocity? <br>Is a prayer nothing more for you than a bargain?<br><br>The large part of this book centers on the questions: at the very beginning of the faith, in the cradle itself, who decides what and how do we worship? What is the ‘proper’ way of worshiping a deity?<br>How is a priesthood born? What are the rules and prohibitions?<br>And most of all: who defines this moral filter from which we could divide justice from injustice?<br><br>Focusing on telling a story how wicked and dangerous priests can become, these mortal messengers of god’s word, how from each next generation that same god’s word can be twisted into something unrecognizable and made to suit selfish needs to whomever currently occupies the highest seat in this newly formed church – especially if when bombarded with questions this deity refuses to answer – we see how easy is for mortal opportunistic desires to become a dogma. <br><br><i>
  <b>
    <u>Prose.</u>
  </b>
</i><br><br>I have seen many complaints about occasional shifting of structural narrative, and I have to say that I do not understand two things.<br>First: why had Erikson decided to introduce this late in the series, practically at the beginning of the end, this change? Was it necessary; to whom actually can we prescribe this change in telling the story - this almost breaking of the fourth wall – and pinching the reader on his nose while doing so?<br>If we prescribe it to <i>
  <b>Kruppe</b>
</i> – then why now? We had this character for four books and he never ventured into something similar in previous installments.<br>But if we somehow manage to prescribe this to newly introduced character, this bard: <i>
  <b>Fisherman</b>
</i> – then I suppose we can understand shifting.<br>But I’m not sure that we can, so overall I’m confused and don’t understand this move Steven decided to make.<br>Second thing that I don’t understand is why people are complaining so much about it? This shift in narrative structure in each chapter is perhaps four paragraphs long. Which means half a page in fifty pages long chapters per average. Which again, in twenty three chapters long book is merely above eleven pages.<br>Now, one may try to build an argument around this and say how ten pages in a thousand pages long book actually managed to disturb or even ruin someone’s experience – but, to be honest, I wouldn’t call that a valid argument. I would actually name that something else.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1519862946i/25140507._SX540_.png" width="" height="" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>
  <b>3.33/5</b>
</i><br><br>Quite generous rating, if you ask me. <br>Book is not only its ending, no matter how breathtaking that ending is.<br><br><i>
  <b>Kharkanas Trilogy<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2167504768?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Forge of Darkness</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1462617190?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Fall of Light</a><br><br>Path to Ascendancy series<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1464500203?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Dancer's Lament</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2189195888?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Deadhouse Landing</a><br><br>Malazan Empire series<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1462615386?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Of Knives</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1462615415?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Return of the Crimson Guard</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1462615435?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Stonewielder</a><br><br>Malazan Book of the Fallen series<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2203966666?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Gardens of the Moon</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2215973985?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Deadhouse Gates</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2226844854?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Memories of Ice</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2242550962" rel="nofollow noopener">Midnight Tides</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2249900119" rel="nofollow noopener">House of Chains</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2265295938?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bonehunters</a><br><a   <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/416454887?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evgeny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evgeny" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny">Evgeny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer416454887" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3581379853669922822">Back in Darujhistan things are back to normal with a lot of parties competing for power, money, influence, or just trying to stay alive. The rules of the game are simple: all means of achieving a goal are fine. A number of people came back to the city and they have no idea what to do in there, which leaves a lot of time for them to ponder on philosophical questions. Meanwhile, in Coral (Black Coral now) Anomander Rake does ... well... something - I still have no clue what he did there.<br/><br/>I rate thi</span>
  <span id="freeText3581379853669922822" style="display:none">Back in Darujhistan things are back to normal with a lot of parties competing for power, money, influence, or just trying to stay alive. The rules of the game are simple: all means of achieving a goal are fine. A number of people came back to the city and they have no idea what to do in there, which leaves a lot of time for them to ponder on philosophical questions. Meanwhile, in Coral (Black Coral now) Anomander Rake does ... well... something - I still have no clue what he did there.<br /><br />I rate this book 2.5 stars rounding down to 2. This was really painful to read. First and foremost, there were a lot of POVs in the book. The author keeps switching between POVs literally on each page which affected my reading pattern the following way: go to the next page, scan it quickly to figure out which POV I read at the moment - it was NOT obvious - go back to the beginning of page and read it carefully to the end. Repeat until finished. Everybody is a philosopher in Malazan universe, which means for every POV switch we have almost full page of deep (or not so deep) thoughts and a couple of lines of action by the end of the page. <br /><br />I finished the book, and still the motivation of the majority of characters is still a mystery to me. I mean, a lot of inner thoughts, some action - and not a word about what motivated this particular character to act the way he acted. I had to read three fourths of the book to finally get some vague ideas of what was about to happen, which by that time begin to bother me. This is 'Show, do not tell' idea taken to the extreme. <br /><br />By the time I finished the book I realized the people in the whole series have no clue what preventive measures are. They all know beforehand that the bad things are coming to them, and nobody lifts his finger to do anything in advance, no kind of preparations until shit really hits the fan. As soon as it happens, people finally start acting - in the very last moment, doing great sacrifices; most of these sacrifices could be easily prevented if somebody bothered to do something (fairly obvious and logical things, too) before the crisis started.<br /><br />Another problem: the whole book is a tale of Kruppe (who, by the way is really boring in this book except for exactly one scene). In each chapter the beginning and the end is told in typical Kruppe manner of speech, but the middle is told in normal way as if the author keeps forgetting is was supposed to be Kruppe who tells the tale. <br /><br />All in all, one of the weakest book in the series. The only emotion I had after I finished it was relief like one after a great physical effort (which it was - sort of). </span>
  <a data-text-id="3581379853669922822" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText18110071487696291309" style="display:none">At this point it's impossible to talk about the plot and characters without spoilers so I won't even try.<br /><br />When I started this many people have said that it's slowest and worst of the Malazan books for 2/3 and they where right but I can't say I was bored. We all know Erikson is absolute master when it comes to writing epic finale but this is where he outdone himself. Convergence of powers in this book is of proportions yet unseen in Malazan world involving gods, ascendants, elder races and mortals and not all of the characters involved get out of it alive and some don't even live to see it. Putting Malazan series on hold until further because I need some time to recover.</span>

<div id="review_1001083579" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1001083579" />
    <a title="Conor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30552336-conor"><img alt="Conor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400278909p2/30552336.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1001083579?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Conor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Conor" href="/user/show/30552336-conor">Conor</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30552336-conor?shelf=malazan">malazan</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1001083579" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6642109318892539437">3.5 Stars. <br/><br/>The painfully slow pace for much of this installment made it the weakest Malazan book in some time, although the typically awesome ending went some way to redeeming it. The return to the city of Darujhistan was cool and I was glad to see some familiar faces (the retired Malazans and the regulars of the Phoenix Inn were both awesome) but these plot-lines were especially slow for much of the book, which was a shame. Although the scene where the Malazan's were attacked in their bar by a </span>
  <span id="freeText6642109318892539437" style="display:none">3.5 Stars. <br /><br />The painfully slow pace for much of this installment made it the weakest Malazan book in some time, although the typically awesome ending went some way to redeeming it. The return to the city of Darujhistan was cool and I was glad to see some familiar faces (the retired Malazans and the regulars of the Phoenix Inn were both awesome) but these plot-lines were especially slow for much of the book, which was a shame. Although the scene where the Malazan's were attacked in their bar by a small army of assassins was one of the most intense, shocking and ultimately emotionally charged (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ tears for Mallet  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> ) in the book and possibly the entire series. I also liked how the Murillio/Harllo/Cutter plotline wove together, with a pair of duels being the highlights <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and Murillio's death was almost on par with Mallet's in hitting me right in the feels  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> . <br /><br />I found Nimander and friends' quest to be one of the more interesting plotline for most of the book although I only started to care about any of the characters a good bit into it. That plotline had a cool resolution that tied it nicely into the overall scheme of things. For some reason I was annoyed by all the comic relief sections in this book: Leff and Scorch, Iskaral Pust (who I normally like) and the entire Trygalle Trade Guild all massively annoyed me, especially the latter who's sections wasted 2 really cool characters in Gruntle and Mappo and seemed to drag on forever. I normally enjoy Erikson's comedic diversions (see Tehol and Bugg) so I don't know why these ones annoyed me so much, maybe it was because the pace of the book as a whole was slow enough without additional diversions. While the Karsa sections were also pretty uneventful (and the whole "why Karsa wants to destroy civilisation" thing was beaten to death) I still found those sections to be enjoyable. Because Witness. Also Karsa's interactions with Samar Dev were really well written to show subtle development in both characters and his bro-mance with fellow badass Traveller was awesome. <br /><br />I found the new characters of Seerdomin and Spinnock Durav to be interesting, although Endest Silann was a regular Buzz Killington. Despite that I found the Black Coral sections to be pretty interesting, owing in large part to the ominous presence of Anomander Rake and the tension built up by his mysterious plans <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ His death at the end of the book was a shocking and brilliantly executed twist that had a really important role in the story  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Overall this was one of the weaker installments in the Malazan franchis but one that still had plenty of stuff going for it. Hopefully the series picks up again with the next book and Erikson is able to deliver his accustomed awesomeness down the stretch.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6642109318892539437" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13077793455987084285">I'll be honest, the first part 60~70% of this book felt pretty dragged out to me. The stories were all over the place with seemingly no way to come together, and I think knowing a lot of the characters at the beginning (for once) made most of the book pretty easy to follow compared to previous books. It was great to finally feel comfortable starting a Malazan book and not being thrown into mass confusion, and it was even better to see characters I love, but the chaos and confusion of starting a </span>
  <span id="freeText13077793455987084285" style="display:none">I'll be honest, the first part 60~70% of this book felt pretty dragged out to me. The stories were all over the place with seemingly no way to come together, and I think knowing a lot of the characters at the beginning (for once) made most of the book pretty easy to follow compared to previous books. It was great to finally feel comfortable starting a Malazan book and not being thrown into mass confusion, and it was even better to see characters I love, but the chaos and confusion of starting a Malazan book is something that has really grown on me and made the series very unique.<br /><br />That being said, this was still a fantastic book and the last 25~30% of the novel was one of the best endings of the series. I think the more story lines seem distant and separate from one another, the more amazed I am at how it all comes together at last. I really cannot express how great that ending was. Throughout the first long 70%, I was just hanging on to my experience that Erikson always delivers, and oh man, he knocked it out of the park with this ending.<br /><br />My favourite story lines were Karsa and Traveller, Cutter (still angry about this name) and of course, the one and only Anomander Rake. I especially loved Rake's POV because throughout the series he is always a major player, but one that tends to lurk in the background as we hear about things he does from other characters. </span>
